<p>A <b>tornado</b> is a rapidly rotating column of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air" title="Air">air</a> that is in contact with both the surface of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Cumulonimbus_cloud" title="Cumulonimbus cloud">cumulonimbus cloud</a> or, in rare cases, the base of a <a href="/wiki/Cumulus_cloud" title="Cumulus cloud">cumulus cloud</a>. They are often referred to as <b>twisters</b>, <b>whirlwinds</b> or <b>cyclones</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> although the word <a href="/wiki/Cyclone" title="Cyclone">cyclone</a> is used in meteorology to name a weather system with a <a href="/wiki/Low-pressure_area" title="Low-pressure area">low-pressure</a> area in the center around which winds blow counterclockwise in the Northern Hemisphere and clockwise in the Southern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Tornadoes come in many shapes and sizes, and they are often visible in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Funnel_cloud" title="Funnel cloud">condensation funnel</a> originating from the base of a cumulonimbus cloud, with a cloud of rotating <a href="/wiki/Debris" title="Debris">debris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dust" title="Dust">dust</a> beneath it. Most tornadoes have wind speeds less than 110 miles per hour (180 km/h), are about 250 feet (80 m) across, and travel a few miles (several kilometers) before dissipating. The <a href="/wiki/Tornado_records#Highest_winds_observed_in_a_tornado" title="Tornado records">most extreme</a> tornadoes can attain wind speeds of more than 300 miles per hour (480 km/h), are more than two miles (3 km) in diameter, and stay on the ground for dozens of miles (more than 100 km).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fastest_wind_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-fastest_wind-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-widest_tornado_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-widest_tornado-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPC_FAQ_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-SPC_FAQ-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Various types of tornadoes include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multiple_vortex_tornado" title="Multiple vortex tornado">multiple vortex tornado</a>, <a href="/wiki/Landspout" title="Landspout">landspout</a> and <a href="/wiki/Waterspout" title="Waterspout">waterspout</a>. Waterspouts are characterized by a spiraling funnel-shaped wind current, connecting to a large cumulus or cumulonimbus cloud. They are generally classified as non-<a href="/wiki/Supercell" title="Supercell">supercellular</a> tornadoes that develop over bodies of water, but there is disagreement over whether to classify them as true tornadoes. These spiraling columns of air frequently develop in tropical areas close to the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>, and are less common at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_latitude" title="High latitude">high latitudes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Other tornado-like phenomena that exist in nature include the <a href="/wiki/Gustnado" title="Gustnado">gustnado</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dust_devil" title="Dust devil">dust devil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fire_whirl" title="Fire whirl">fire whirls</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Steam_devil" title="Steam devil">steam devil</a>.</p>
<p>Tornadoes have been observed and documented on every continent except Antarctica. However, the vast majority of tornadoes occur in the <a href="/wiki/Tornado_Alley" title="Tornado Alley">Tornado Alley</a> region of the United States, although they can occur nearly anywhere in North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_News_1_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Science_News_1-7">[7]</a></sup> They also occasionally occur in south-central and eastern Asia, northern and east-central South America, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa</a>, northwestern and southeast Europe, western and southeastern Australia, and New Zealand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB_tornado_climatology_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB_tornado_climatology-8">[8]</a></sup> Tornadoes can be detected before or as they occur through the use of <a href="/wiki/Pulse-Doppler_radar" title="Pulse-Doppler radar">Pulse-Doppler radar</a> by recognizing patterns in velocity and reflectivity data, such as <a href="/wiki/Hook_echo" title="Hook echo">hook echoes</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Debris_ball" title="Debris ball">debris balls</a>, as well as through the efforts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Storm_spotter" title="Storm spotter">storm spotters</a>.</p>
<p>There are several scales for rating the strength of tornadoes. The <a href="/wiki/Fujita_scale" title="Fujita scale">Fujita scale</a> rates tornadoes by damage caused and has been replaced in some countries by the updated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enhanced_Fujita_Scale" title="Enhanced Fujita Scale">Enhanced Fujita Scale</a>. An F0 or EF0 tornado, the weakest category, damages trees, but not substantial structures. An F5 or EF5 tornado, the strongest category, rips buildings off their foundations and can deform large <a href="/wiki/Skyscraper" title="Skyscraper">skyscrapers</a>. The similar <a href="/wiki/TORRO_scale" title="TORRO scale">TORRO scale</a> ranges from a T0 for extremely weak tornadoes to T11 for the most powerful known tornadoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Doppler <a href="/wiki/Weather_radar" title="Weather radar">radar</a> data, <a href="/wiki/Photogrammetry" title="Photogrammetry">photogrammetry</a>, and ground swirl patterns (<a href="/wiki/Cycloid" title="Cycloid">cycloidal</a> marks) may also be analyzed to determine intensity and assign a rating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EF_SPC_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-EF_SPC-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dszpics1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="679" data-file-width="1024" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Dszpics1.jpg/300px-Dszpics1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Dszpics1.jpg/450px-Dszpics1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Dszpics1.jpg/600px-Dszpics1.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dszpics1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A tornado near <a href="/wiki/Anadarko,_Oklahoma" title="Anadarko, Oklahoma">Anadarko, Oklahoma</a>. The <i>funnel</i> is the thin tube reaching from the cloud to the ground. The lower part of this tornado is surrounded by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transparency_(optics)" title="Transparency (optics)">translucent</a> dust cloud, kicked up by the tornado's strong winds at the surface. The wind of the tornado has a much wider radius than the funnel itself.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Funnel_near_Eads,_Colorado.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1389" data-file-width="2471" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Funnel_near_Eads%2C_Colorado.jpg/300px-Funnel_near_Eads%2C_Colorado.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Funnel_near_Eads%2C_Colorado.jpg/450px-Funnel_near_Eads%2C_Colorado.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Funnel_near_Eads%2C_Colorado.jpg/600px-Funnel_near_Eads%2C_Colorado.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Funnel_near_Eads,_Colorado.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A tornado near Eads, Colorado.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AllTdots.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1875" data-file-width="2500" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/AllTdots.png/300px-AllTdots.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/AllTdots.png/450px-AllTdots.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/AllTdots.png/600px-AllTdots.png 2x" width="300"/></a>

</div>
</div>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The word <i>tornado</i> is an altered form of the Spanish word <i>tronada</i>, which means "thunderstorm". This in turn was taken from the Latin <i>tonare</i>, meaning "to thunder". It most likely reached its present form through a combination of the Spanish <i>tronada</i> and <i>tornar</i> ("to turn"); however, this may be a <a href="/wiki/Folk_etymology" title="Folk etymology">folk etymology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etymology_1_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-etymology_1-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etymology_2_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-etymology_2-13">[13]</a></sup> A tornado is also commonly referred to as a "twister", and is also sometimes referred to by the old-fashioned colloquial term <i>cyclone</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TT_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-TT-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nssl_faq_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-nssl_faq-15">[15]</a></sup> The term "cyclone" is used as a synonym for "tornado" in the often-aired 1939 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)" title="The Wizard of Oz (1939 film)">The Wizard of Oz</a></i>. The term "twister" is also used in that film, along with being the title of the 1996 tornado-related film <i><a href="/wiki/Twister_(1996_film)" title="Twister (1996 film)">Twister</a></i>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seymour_Texas_Tornado.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="334" data-file-width="521" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Seymour_Texas_Tornado.jpg/220px-Seymour_Texas_Tornado.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Seymour_Texas_Tornado.jpg/330px-Seymour_Texas_Tornado.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Seymour_Texas_Tornado.jpg/440px-Seymour_Texas_Tornado.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Seymour_Texas_Tornado.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A tornado near <a href="/wiki/Seymour,_Texas" title="Seymour, Texas">Seymour, Texas</a> 1979</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A tornado is "a violently rotating column of air, in contact with the ground, either pendant from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cumuliform_cloud" title="Cumuliform cloud">cumuliform cloud</a> or underneath a cumuliform cloud, and often (but not always) visible as a funnel cloud".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glossary_of_Meteorology_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glossary_of_Meteorology-16">[16]</a></sup> For a vortex to be classified as a tornado, it must be in contact with both the ground and the cloud base. Scientists have not yet created a complete definition of the word; for example, there is disagreement as to whether separate touchdowns of the same funnel constitute separate tornadoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPC_FAQ_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-SPC_FAQ-5">[5]</a></sup> <i>Tornado</i> refers to the <a href="/wiki/Vortex" title="Vortex">vortex</a> of wind, not the condensation cloud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Advanced_Spotter_Guide_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Advanced_Spotter_Guide-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado.3F_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-tornado.3F-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Funnel_cloud">Funnel cloud</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Funnel_cloud" title="Funnel cloud">Funnel cloud</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tornado_with_no_funnel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="920" data-file-width="1018" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Tornado_with_no_funnel.jpg/220px-Tornado_with_no_funnel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Tornado_with_no_funnel.jpg/330px-Tornado_with_no_funnel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Tornado_with_no_funnel.jpg/440px-Tornado_with_no_funnel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tornado_with_no_funnel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This tornado has no funnel cloud; however, the rotating dust cloud indicates that strong winds are occurring at the surface, and thus it is a true tornado.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A tornado is not necessarily visible; however, the intense low pressure caused by the high wind speeds (as described by <a href="/wiki/Bernoulli%27s_principle" title="Bernoulli's principle">Bernoulli's principle</a>) and rapid rotation (due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyclostrophic_balance" title="Cyclostrophic balance">cyclostrophic balance</a>) usually cause <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a> in the air to condense into cloud droplets due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adiabatic_cooling" title="Adiabatic cooling">adiabatic cooling</a>. This results in the formation of a visible funnel cloud or condensation funnel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Renno_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Renno-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is some disagreement over the definition of a funnel cloud and a condensation funnel. According to the <i>Glossary of Meteorology</i>, a funnel cloud is any rotating cloud pendant from a cumulus or cumulonimbus, and thus most tornadoes are included under this definition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Among many meteorologists, the 'funnel cloud' term is strictly defined as a rotating cloud which is not associated with strong winds at the surface, and condensation funnel is a broad term for any rotating cloud below a cumuliform cloud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPC_FAQ_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-SPC_FAQ-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Tornadoes often begin as funnel clouds with no associated strong winds at the surface, and not all funnel clouds evolve into tornadoes. Most tornadoes produce strong winds at the surface while the visible funnel is still above the ground, so it is difficult to discern the difference between a funnel cloud and a tornado from a distance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPC_FAQ_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-SPC_FAQ-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Outbreaks_and_families">Outbreaks and families</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Tornado_family" title="Tornado family">Tornado family</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tornado_outbreak" title="Tornado outbreak">tornado outbreak</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tornado_outbreak_sequence" title="Tornado outbreak sequence">tornado outbreak sequence</a></div>
<p>Occasionally, a single storm will produce more than one tornado, either simultaneously or in succession. Multiple tornadoes produced by the same <a href="/wiki/Storm_cell" title="Storm cell">storm cell</a> are referred to as a "tornado family".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Several tornadoes are sometimes spawned from the same large-scale storm system. If there is no break in activity, this is considered a tornado outbreak (although the term "tornado outbreak" has various definitions). A period of several successive days with tornado outbreaks in the same general area (spawned by multiple weather systems) is a tornado outbreak sequence, occasionally called an extended tornado outbreak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glossary_of_Meteorology_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Glossary_of_Meteorology-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-significant_tornadoes_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-significant_tornadoes-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size_and_shape">Size and shape</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Binger_Oklahoma_Tornado.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="333" data-file-width="533" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Binger_Oklahoma_Tornado.jpg/220px-Binger_Oklahoma_Tornado.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Binger_Oklahoma_Tornado.jpg/330px-Binger_Oklahoma_Tornado.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Binger_Oklahoma_Tornado.jpg/440px-Binger_Oklahoma_Tornado.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Binger_Oklahoma_Tornado.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A wedge tornado, nearly a mile wide, which hit <a href="/wiki/May_1981_tornado_outbreak" title="May 1981 tornado outbreak">Binger, Oklahoma in 1981</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most tornadoes take on the appearance of a narrow <a href="/wiki/Funnel" title="Funnel">funnel</a>, a few hundred yards (meters) across, with a small cloud of debris near the ground. Tornadoes may be obscured completely by rain or dust. These tornadoes are especially dangerous, as even experienced meteorologists might not see them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handy_Weather_Answer_Book_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Handy_Weather_Answer_Book-24">[24]</a></sup> Tornadoes can appear in many shapes and sizes.</p>
<p>Small, relatively weak landspouts may be visible only as a small swirl of dust on the ground. Although the condensation funnel may not extend all the way to the ground, if associated surface winds are greater than 40 mph (64 km/h), the circulation is considered a tornado.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Advanced_Spotter_Guide_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Advanced_Spotter_Guide-17">[17]</a></sup> A tornado with a nearly cylindrical profile and relative low height is sometimes referred to as a "stovepipe" tornado. Large single-vortex tornadoes can look like large <a href="/wiki/Wedge_(mechanical_device)" title="Wedge (mechanical device)">wedges</a> stuck into the ground, and so are known as "wedge tornadoes" or "wedges". The "stovepipe" classification is also used for this type of tornado if it otherwise fits that profile. A wedge can be so wide that it appears to be a block of dark clouds, wider than the distance from the cloud base to the ground. Even experienced storm observers may not be able to tell the difference between a low-hanging cloud and a wedge tornado from a distance. Many, but not all major tornadoes are wedges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wedge_tornado_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-wedge_tornado-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roping_tornado.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="911" data-file-width="700" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Roping_tornado.jpg/220px-Roping_tornado.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Roping_tornado.jpg/330px-Roping_tornado.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Roping_tornado.jpg/440px-Roping_tornado.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roping_tornado.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A rope tornado in its dissipating stage, <a href="/wiki/Tecumseh,_Oklahoma" title="Tecumseh, Oklahoma">Tecumseh, Oklahoma</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Tornadoes in the dissipating stage can resemble narrow tubes or ropes, and often curl or twist into complex shapes. These tornadoes are said to be "roping out", or becoming a "rope tornado". When they rope out, the length of their funnel increases, which forces the winds within the funnel to weaken due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_of_angular_momentum" title="Conservation of angular momentum">conservation of angular momentum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebible_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-thebible-26">[26]</a></sup> Multiple-vortex tornadoes can appear as a family of swirls circling a common center, or they may be completely obscured by condensation, dust, and debris, appearing to be a single funnel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rope_tornado_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-rope_tornado-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the United States, tornadoes are around 500 feet (150 m) across on average and travel on the ground for 5 miles (8.0 km).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handy_Weather_Answer_Book_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Handy_Weather_Answer_Book-24">[24]</a></sup> However, there is a wide range of tornado sizes. Weak tornadoes, or strong yet dissipating tornadoes, can be exceedingly narrow, sometimes only a few feet or couple meters across. One tornado was reported to have a damage path only 7 feet (2.1 m) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handy_Weather_Answer_Book_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-Handy_Weather_Answer_Book-24">[24]</a></sup> On the other end of the spectrum, wedge tornadoes can have a damage path a mile (1.6 km) wide or more. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/May_2004_tornado_outbreak_sequence" title="May 2004 tornado outbreak sequence">tornado that affected Hallam, Nebraska</a> on May 22, 2004, was up to 2.5 miles (4.0 km) wide at the ground, and <a href="/wiki/2013_El_Reno_tornado" title="2013 El Reno tornado">a tornado in El Reno, Oklahoma</a> on May 31, 2013 was approximately 2.6 miles (4.2 km) wide, the widest on record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-widest_tornado_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-widest_tornado-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>In terms of path length, the <a href="/wiki/Tri-State_Tornado" title="Tri-State Tornado">Tri-State Tornado</a>, which affected parts of <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a> on March 18, 1925, was on the ground continuously for 219 miles (352 km). Many tornadoes which appear to have path lengths of 100 miles (160 km) or longer are composed of a family of tornadoes which have formed in quick succession; however, there is no substantial evidence that this occurred in the case of the Tri-State Tornado.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-significant_tornadoes_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-significant_tornadoes-22">[22]</a></sup> In fact, modern reanalysis of the path suggests that the tornado may have begun 15 miles (24 km) further west than previously thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Appearance">Appearance</span></h3>
<p>Tornadoes can have a wide range of colors, depending on the environment in which they form. Those that form in dry environments can be nearly invisible, marked only by swirling debris at the base of the funnel. Condensation funnels that pick up little or no debris can be gray to white. While traveling over a body of water (as a waterspout), tornadoes can turn white or even blue. Slow-moving funnels, which ingest a considerable amount of debris and dirt, are usually darker, taking on the color of debris. Tornadoes in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a> can turn red because of the reddish tint of the soil, and tornadoes in mountainous areas can travel over snow-covered ground, turning white.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handy_Weather_Answer_Book_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-Handy_Weather_Answer_Book-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waurika_Oklahoma_Tornado_Back_and_Front.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="636" data-file-width="498" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Waurika_Oklahoma_Tornado_Back_and_Front.jpg/220px-Waurika_Oklahoma_Tornado_Back_and_Front.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Waurika_Oklahoma_Tornado_Back_and_Front.jpg/330px-Waurika_Oklahoma_Tornado_Back_and_Front.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Waurika_Oklahoma_Tornado_Back_and_Front.jpg/440px-Waurika_Oklahoma_Tornado_Back_and_Front.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Waurika_Oklahoma_Tornado_Back_and_Front.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Photographs of the <a href="/wiki/Waurika,_Oklahoma" title="Waurika, Oklahoma">Waurika, Oklahoma</a> tornado of May 30, 1976, taken at nearly the same time by two photographers. In the top picture, the tornado is lit with the sunlight focused from behind the <a href="/wiki/Camera" title="Camera">camera</a>, thus the funnel appears bluish. In the lower image, where the camera is facing the opposite direction, the sun is behind the tornado, giving it a dark appearance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PD_tornado_images_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-PD_tornado_images-30">[30]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Lighting conditions are a major factor in the appearance of a tornado. A tornado which is "<a href="/wiki/Backlighting_(lighting_design)" title="Backlighting (lighting design)">back-lit</a>" (viewed with the sun behind it) appears very dark. The same tornado, viewed with the sun at the observer's back, may appear gray or brilliant white. Tornadoes which occur near the time of sunset can be many different colors, appearing in hues of yellow, orange, and pink.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TT_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-TT-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-target_tornado_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-target_tornado-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>Dust kicked up by the winds of the parent thunderstorm, heavy rain and hail, and the darkness of night are all factors which can reduce the visibility of tornadoes. Tornadoes occurring in these conditions are especially dangerous, since only <a href="/wiki/Weather_radar" title="Weather radar">weather radar</a> observations, or possibly the sound of an approaching tornado, serve as any warning to those in the storm's path. Most significant tornadoes form under the storm's <i>updraft base</i>, which is rain-free,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> making them visible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Also, most tornadoes occur in the late afternoon, when the bright sun can penetrate even the thickest clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-significant_tornadoes_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-significant_tornadoes-22">[22]</a></sup> Night-time tornadoes are often illuminated by frequent lightning.</p>
<p>There is mounting evidence, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doppler_On_Wheels" title="Doppler On Wheels">Doppler On Wheels</a> mobile radar images and eyewitness accounts, that most tornadoes have a clear, calm center with extremely low pressure, akin to the <a href="/wiki/Eye_(cyclone)" title="Eye (cyclone)">eye</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">tropical cyclones</a>. Lightning is said to be the source of illumination for those who claim to have seen the interior of a tornado.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_News_2_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Science_News_2-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inside_eyewitness_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-inside_eyewitness-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rotation">Rotation</span></h3>
<p>Tornadoes normally rotate <a href="/wiki/Cyclone" title="Cyclone">cyclonically</a> (when viewed from above, this is counterclockwise in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_hemisphere" title="Northern hemisphere">northern hemisphere</a> and clockwise in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_hemisphere" title="Southern hemisphere">southern</a>). While large-scale storms always rotate cyclonically due to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect" title="Coriolis effect">Coriolis effect</a>, thunderstorms and tornadoes are so small that the direct influence of the Coriolis effect is unimportant, as indicated by their large <a href="/wiki/Rossby_number" title="Rossby number">Rossby numbers</a>. Supercells and tornadoes rotate cyclonically in numerical simulations even when the Coriolis effect is neglected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Origin_of_Updraft_Rotation_in_Supercells_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Origin_of_Updraft_Rotation_in_Supercells-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rotation_and_Propagation_of_Simulated_Supercells_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rotation_and_Propagation_of_Simulated_Supercells-38">[38]</a></sup> Low-level <a href="/wiki/Mesocyclone" title="Mesocyclone">mesocyclones</a> and tornadoes owe their rotation to complex processes within the supercell and ambient environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tornado_Development_and_Decay_within_a_Supercell_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tornado_Development_and_Decay_within_a_Supercell-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Approximately 1 percent of tornadoes rotate in an anticyclonic direction in the northern hemisphere. Typically, systems as weak as landspouts and gustnadoes can rotate anticyclonically, and usually only those which form on the anticyclonic shear side of the descending <a href="/wiki/Rear_flank_downdraft" title="Rear flank downdraft">rear flank downdraft</a> (RFD) in a cyclonic supercell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Recent_Example_of_an_anticyclonic_tornado_in_El_Reno.2C_OK_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Recent_Example_of_an_anticyclonic_tornado_in_El_Reno.2C_OK-40">[40]</a></sup> On rare occasions, <a href="/wiki/Anticyclonic_tornado" title="Anticyclonic tornado">anticyclonic tornadoes</a> form in association with the mesoanticyclone of an anticyclonic supercell, in the same manner as the typical cyclonic tornado, or as a companion tornado either as a satellite tornado or associated with anticyclonic eddies within a supercell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sunnyvale_Tornado_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sunnyvale_Tornado-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sound_and_seismology">Sound and seismology</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tornado_infrasound_sources.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="260" data-file-width="328" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Tornado_infrasound_sources.png/220px-Tornado_infrasound_sources.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/63/Tornado_infrasound_sources.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tornado_infrasound_sources.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An illustration of generation of infrasound in tornadoes by the <a href="/wiki/Earth_System_Research_Laboratory" title="Earth System Research Laboratory">Earth System Research Laboratory</a>'s Infrasound Program</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Tornadoes emit widely on the <a href="/wiki/Acoustics" title="Acoustics">acoustics</a> <a href="/wiki/Audio_frequency" title="Audio frequency">spectrum</a> and the sounds are caused by multiple mechanisms. Various sounds of tornadoes have been reported, mostly related to familiar sounds for the witness and generally some variation of a whooshing roar. Popularly reported sounds include a freight train, rushing rapids or waterfall, a nearby jet engine, or combinations of these. Many tornadoes are not audible from much distance; the nature of and the propagation distance of the audible sound depends on atmospheric conditions and topography.</p>
<p>The winds of the tornado vortex and of constituent turbulent <a href="/wiki/Eddy_(fluid_dynamics)" title="Eddy (fluid dynamics)">eddies</a>, as well as airflow interaction with the surface and debris, contribute to the sounds. Funnel clouds also produce sounds. Funnel clouds and small tornadoes are reported as whistling, whining, humming, or the buzzing of innumerable bees or electricity, or more or less harmonic, whereas many tornadoes are reported as a continuous, deep rumbling, or an irregular sound of "noise".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_music_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_music-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since many tornadoes are audible only when very near, sound is not to be thought of as a reliable warning signal for a tornado. Tornadoes are also not the only source of such sounds in severe thunderstorms; any strong, damaging wind, a severe hail volley, or continuous thunder in a thunderstorm may produce a roaring sound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sound_obs_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-sound_obs-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Tornadoes also produce identifiable inaudible <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infrasonic" title="Infrasonic">infrasonic</a> signatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_infrasonics_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_infrasonics-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Unlike audible signatures, tornadic signatures have been isolated; due to the long distance propagation of low-frequency sound, efforts are ongoing to develop tornado prediction and detection devices with additional value in understanding tornado morphology, dynamics, and creation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-field_programs_history_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-field_programs_history-45">[45]</a></sup> Tornadoes also produce a detectable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seismic" title="Seismic">seismic</a> signature, and research continues on isolating it and understanding the process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_seismic_signal_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_seismic_signal-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Electromagnetic,_lightning,_and_other_effects"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Electromagnetic.2C_lightning.2C_and_other_effects">Electromagnetic, lightning, and other effects</span></h3>
<p>Tornadoes emit on the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">electromagnetic spectrum</a>, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sferics" title="Sferics">sferics</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E-field" title="E-field">E-field</a> effects detected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-field_programs_history_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-field_programs_history-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-in_situ_history_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-in_situ_history-48">[48]</a></sup> There are observed correlations between tornadoes and patterns of lightning. Tornadic storms do not contain more lightning than other storms and some tornadic cells never produce lightning at all. More often than not, overall cloud-to-ground (CG) lightning activity decreases as a tornado touches the surface and returns to the baseline level when the tornado dissipates. In many cases, intense tornadoes and thunderstorms exhibit an increased and anomalous dominance of positive polarity CG discharges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CG_tor_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-CG_tor-49">[49]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetics" title="Electromagnetics">Electromagnetics</a> and lightning have little or nothing to do directly with what drives tornadoes (tornadoes are basically a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermodynamic" title="Thermodynamic">thermodynamic</a> phenomenon), although there are likely connections with the storm and environment affecting both phenomena.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Luminosity" title="Luminosity">Luminosity</a> has been reported in the past and is probably due to misidentification of external light sources such as lightning, city lights, and <a href="/wiki/Power_flash" title="Power flash">power flashes</a> from broken lines, as internal sources are now uncommonly reported and are not known to ever have been recorded. In addition to winds, tornadoes also exhibit changes in atmospheric variables such as <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moisture" title="Moisture">moisture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">pressure</a>. For example, on June 24, 2003 near <a href="/wiki/Manchester,_South_Dakota" title="Manchester, South Dakota">Manchester, South Dakota</a>, a probe measured a 100 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">mbar</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">hPa</a>) (2.95 <a href="/wiki/Inch_of_mercury" title="Inch of mercury">inHg</a>) pressure decrease. The pressure dropped gradually as the vortex approached then dropped extremely rapidly to 850 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">mbar</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">hPa</a>) (25.10 <a href="/wiki/Inch_of_mercury" title="Inch of mercury">inHg</a>) in the core of the violent tornado before rising rapidly as the vortex moved away, resulting in a V-shape pressure trace. Temperature tends to decrease and moisture content to increase in the immediate vicinity of a tornado.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manchester_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manchester-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_cycle">Life cycle</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Tornadogenesis" title="Tornadogenesis">Tornadogenesis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dimmit_Sequence.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1389" data-file-width="700" height="437" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Dimmit_Sequence.jpg/220px-Dimmit_Sequence.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Dimmit_Sequence.jpg/330px-Dimmit_Sequence.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Dimmit_Sequence.jpg/440px-Dimmit_Sequence.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dimmit_Sequence.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A sequence of images showing the birth of a tornado. First, the rotating cloud base lowers. This lowering becomes a funnel, which continues descending while winds build near the surface, kicking up dust and debris and causing damage. As the pressure continues to drop, the visible funnel extends to the ground. This tornado, near <a href="/wiki/Dimmitt,_Texas" title="Dimmitt, Texas">Dimmitt, Texas</a>, was one of the best-observed violent tornadoes in history.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supercell_relationship">Supercell relationship</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Supercell" title="Supercell">Supercell</a></div>
<p>Tornadoes often develop from a class of thunderstorms known as supercells. Supercells contain <a href="/wiki/Mesocyclone" title="Mesocyclone">mesocyclones</a>, an area of organized rotation a few miles up in the atmosphere, usually 1–6 miles (1.6–9.7 kilometres) across. Most intense tornadoes (EF3 to EF5 on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enhanced_Fujita_Scale" title="Enhanced Fujita Scale">Enhanced Fujita Scale</a>) develop from supercells. In addition to tornadoes, very heavy rain, frequent lightning, strong wind gusts, and hail are common in such storms.</p>
<p>Most tornadoes from supercells follow a recognizable life cycle. That begins when increasing rainfall drags with it an area of quickly descending air known as the rear flank downdraft (RFD). This downdraft accelerates as it approaches the ground, and drags the supercell's rotating mesocyclone towards the ground with it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Advanced_Spotter_Guide_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Advanced_Spotter_Guide-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span></h3>
<p>As the mesocyclone lowers below the cloud base, it begins to take in cool, moist air from the downdraft region of the storm. The convergence of warm air in the updraft and cool air causes a rotating wall cloud to form. The RFD also focuses the mesocyclone's base, causing it to draw air from a smaller and smaller area on the ground. As the updraft intensifies, it creates an area of low pressure at the surface. This pulls the focused mesocyclone down, in the form of a visible condensation funnel. As the funnel descends, the RFD also reaches the ground, fanning outward and creating a gust front that can cause severe damage a considerable distance from the tornado. Usually, the funnel cloud begins causing damage on the ground (becoming a tornado) within a few minutes of the RFD reaching the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Advanced_Spotter_Guide_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-Advanced_Spotter_Guide-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maturity">Maturity</span></h3>
<p>Initially, the tornado has a good source of warm, moist air <a href="/wiki/Inflow_(meteorology)" title="Inflow (meteorology)">flowing inward</a> to power it, and it grows until it reaches the "mature stage". This can last anywhere from a few minutes to more than an hour, and during that time a tornado often causes the most damage, and in rare cases can be more than one mile (1.6 km) across. The low pressured atmosphere at the base of the tornado is essential to the endurance of the system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Meanwhile, the RFD, now an area of cool surface winds, begins to wrap around the tornado, cutting off the inflow of warm air which previously fed the tornado.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Advanced_Spotter_Guide_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-Advanced_Spotter_Guide-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dissipation">Dissipation</span></h3>
<p>As the RFD completely wraps around and chokes off the tornado's air supply, the vortex begins to weaken, and become thin and rope-like. This is the "dissipating stage", often lasting no more than a few minutes, after which the tornado ends. During this stage the shape of the tornado becomes highly influenced by the winds of the parent storm, and can be blown into fantastic patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-significant_tornadoes_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-significant_tornadoes-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PD_tornado_images_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-PD_tornado_images-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-target_tornado_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-target_tornado-31">[31]</a></sup> Even though the tornado is dissipating, it is still capable of causing damage. The storm is contracting into a rope-like tube and, due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_of_angular_momentum" title="Conservation of angular momentum">conservation of angular momentum</a>, winds can increase at this point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thebible_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-thebible-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the tornado enters the dissipating stage, its associated mesocyclone often weakens as well, as the rear flank downdraft cuts off the inflow powering it. Sometimes, in intense supercells, tornadoes can develop <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cycle" title="wiktionary:cycle">cyclically</a>. As the first mesocyclone and associated tornado dissipate, the storm's inflow may be concentrated into a new area closer to the center of the storm and possibly feed a new mesocyclone. If a new mesocyclone develops, the cycle may start again, producing one or more new tornadoes. Occasionally, the old (occluded) mesocyclone and the new mesocyclone produce a tornado at the same time.</p>
<p>Although this is a widely accepted theory for how most tornadoes form, live, and die, it does not explain the formation of smaller tornadoes, such as landspouts, long-lived tornadoes, or tornadoes with multiple vortices. These each have different mechanisms which influence their development—however, most tornadoes follow a pattern similar to this one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornadogenesis_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-tornadogenesis-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Multiple_vortex">Multiple vortex</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Multiple-vortex_tornado" title="Multiple-vortex tornado">Multiple-vortex tornado</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1957_Dallas_multi-vortex_1_edited.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="465" data-file-width="700" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/1957_Dallas_multi-vortex_1_edited.JPG/220px-1957_Dallas_multi-vortex_1_edited.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/1957_Dallas_multi-vortex_1_edited.JPG/330px-1957_Dallas_multi-vortex_1_edited.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/1957_Dallas_multi-vortex_1_edited.JPG/440px-1957_Dallas_multi-vortex_1_edited.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1957_Dallas_multi-vortex_1_edited.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A multiple-vortex <a href="/wiki/Early-April_1957_tornado_outbreak_sequence" title="Early-April 1957 tornado outbreak sequence">tornado</a> outside <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dallas,_Texas" title="Dallas, Texas">Dallas, Texas</a> on April 2, 1957.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <i>multiple-vortex tornado</i> is a type of tornado in which two or more columns of spinning air rotate about their own axis and at the same time around a common center. A multi-vortex structure can occur in almost any circulation, but is very often observed in intense tornadoes. These vortices often create small areas of heavier damage along the main tornado path.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPC_FAQ_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-SPC_FAQ-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Advanced_Spotter_Guide_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-Advanced_Spotter_Guide-17">[17]</a></sup> This is a phenomenon that is distinct from a <a href="/wiki/Satellite_tornado" title="Satellite tornado">satellite tornado</a>, which is a smaller tornado which forms very near a large, strong tornado contained within the same mesocyclone. The satellite tornado may appear to "<a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbit</a>" the larger tornado (hence the name), giving the appearance of one, large multi-vortex tornado. However, a satellite tornado is a distinct circulation, and is much smaller than the main funnel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPC_FAQ_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-SPC_FAQ-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Waterspout">Waterspout</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Waterspout" title="Waterspout">Waterspout</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trombe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1630" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Trombe.jpg/220px-Trombe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Trombe.jpg/330px-Trombe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Trombe.jpg/440px-Trombe.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trombe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A waterspout near the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Keys" title="Florida Keys">Florida Keys</a> in 1969.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <i>waterspout</i> is defined by the <a href="/wiki/National_Weather_Service" title="National Weather Service">National Weather Service</a> as a tornado over water. However, researchers typically distinguish "fair weather" waterspouts from tornadic waterspouts. Fair weather waterspouts are less severe but far more common, and are similar to <a href="/wiki/Dust_devil" title="Dust devil">dust devils</a> and <a href="/wiki/Landspout" title="Landspout">landspouts</a>. They form at the bases of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cumulus_congestus" title="Cumulus congestus">cumulus congestus</a> clouds over tropical and subtropical waters. They have relatively weak winds, smooth <a href="/wiki/Laminar_flow" title="Laminar flow">laminar</a> walls, and typically travel very slowly. They occur most commonly in the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Keys" title="Florida Keys">Florida Keys</a> and in the northern <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Sea" title="Adriatic Sea">Adriatic Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USA_Today_1_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-USA_Today_1-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USA_Today_2_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-USA_Today_2-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> In contrast, tornadic waterspouts are stronger tornadoes over water. They form over water similarly to mesocyclonic tornadoes, or are stronger tornadoes which cross over water. Since they form from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Severe_thunderstorm" title="Severe thunderstorm">severe thunderstorms</a> and can be far more intense, faster, and longer-lived than fair weather waterspouts, they are more dangerous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> In official tornado statistics, waterspouts are generally not counted unless they affect land, though some European weather agencies count waterspouts and tornadoes together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPC_FAQ_5-6"><a href="#cite_note-SPC_FAQ-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESWD_definitions_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESWD_definitions-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Landspout">Landspout</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Landspout" title="Landspout">Landspout</a></div>
<p>A <i>landspout</i>, or <i>dust-tube tornado</i>, is a tornado not associated with a mesocyclone. The name stems from their characterization as a "fair weather waterspout on land". Waterspouts and landspouts share many defining characteristics, including relative weakness, short lifespan, and a small, smooth condensation funnel which often does not reach the surface. Landspouts also create a distinctively laminar cloud of dust when they make contact with the ground, due to their differing mechanics from true mesoform tornadoes. Though usually weaker than classic tornadoes, they can produce strong winds which could cause serious damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPC_FAQ_5-7"><a href="#cite_note-SPC_FAQ-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Advanced_Spotter_Guide_17-6"><a href="#cite_note-Advanced_Spotter_Guide-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Similar_circulations">Similar circulations</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gustnado">Gustnado</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gustnado" title="Gustnado">Gustnado</a></div>
<p>A <i>gustnado</i>, or <i>gust front tornado</i>, is a small, vertical swirl associated with a <a href="/wiki/Outflow_boundary" title="Outflow boundary">gust front</a> or <a href="/wiki/Downburst" title="Downburst">downburst</a>. Because they are not connected with a cloud base, there is some debate as to whether or not gustnadoes are tornadoes. They are formed when fast moving cold, dry outflow air from a <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">thunderstorm</a> is blown through a mass of stationary, warm, moist air near the outflow boundary, resulting in a "rolling" effect (often exemplified through a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roll_cloud" title="Roll cloud">roll cloud</a>). If low level <a href="/wiki/Wind_shear" title="Wind shear">wind shear</a> is strong enough, the rotation can be turned vertically or diagonally and make contact with the ground. The result is a gustnado.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPC_FAQ_5-8"><a href="#cite_note-SPC_FAQ-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gustnado_AMS_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-gustnado_AMS-59">[59]</a></sup> They usually cause small areas of heavier rotational wind damage among areas of straight-line wind damage.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dust_devil">Dust devil</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dust_devil" title="Dust devil">Dust devil</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dust_devil.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="2448" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Dust_devil.jpg/220px-Dust_devil.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Dust_devil.jpg/330px-Dust_devil.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Dust_devil.jpg/440px-Dust_devil.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dust_devil.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A dust devil in <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <i>dust devil</i> (also known as a whirlwind) resembles a tornado in that it is a vertical swirling column of air. However, they form under clear skies and are no stronger than the weakest tornadoes. They form when a strong convective updraft is formed near the ground on a hot day. If there is enough low level wind shear, the column of hot, rising air can develop a small cyclonic motion that can be seen near the ground. They are not considered tornadoes because they form during fair weather and are not associated with any clouds. However, they can, on occasion, result in major damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handy_Weather_Answer_Book_24-4"><a href="#cite_note-Handy_Weather_Answer_Book-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dust_devil_injury_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-dust_devil_injury-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fire_whirls">Fire whirls</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fire_whirl" title="Fire whirl">Fire whirl</a></div>
<p>Small-scale, tornado-like circulations can occur near any intense surface heat source. Those that occur near intense <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">wildfires</a> are called <i>fire whirls</i>. They are not considered tornadoes, except in the rare case where they connect to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyrocumulus" title="Pyrocumulus">pyrocumulus</a> or other cumuliform cloud above. Fire whirls usually are not as strong as tornadoes associated with thunderstorms. They can, however, produce significant damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-significant_tornadoes_22-4"><a href="#cite_note-significant_tornadoes-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Steam_devils">Steam devils</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Steam_devil" title="Steam devil">Steam devil</a></div>
<p>A <i>steam devil</i> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rotating" title="Rotating">rotating</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Updraft" title="Updraft">updraft</a> between 50 and 200 meters wide that involves steam or smoke. These formations do not involve high wind speeds, only completing a few rotations per minute. Steam devils are very rare. They most often form from smoke issuing from a power plant's smokestack. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hot_springs" title="Hot springs">Hot springs</a> and deserts may also be suitable locations for a tighter, faster-rotating steam devil to form. The phenomenon can occur over water, when cold arctic air passes over relatively warm water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handy_Weather_Answer_Book_24-5"><a href="#cite_note-Handy_Weather_Answer_Book-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Intensity_and_damage">Intensity and damage</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tornado_intensity_and_damage" title="Tornado intensity and damage">Tornado intensity and damage</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_Fujita_scale" title="Enhanced Fujita scale">Enhanced Fujita scale</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fujita_scale" title="Fujita scale">Fujita scale</a>, and <a href="/wiki/TORRO_scale" title="TORRO scale">TORRO scale</a></div>
<table class="wikitable" style="float: right;">
<caption>Tornado rating classifications<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-significant_tornadoes_22-5"><a href="#cite_note-significant_tornadoes-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></caption>
<tr>
<th style="background: #e0e0ff;"><b>F0<br/>
EF0</b></th>
<th style="background: #e0e0ff;"><b>F1<br/>
EF1</b></th>
<th style="background: #e0e0ff;"><b>F2<br/>
EF2</b></th>
<th style="background: #e0e0ff;"><b>F3<br/>
EF3</b></th>
<th style="background: #e0e0ff;"><b>F4<br/>
EF4</b></th>
<th style="background: #e0e0ff;"><b>F5<br/>
EF5</b></th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="background: #fd0;">Weak</td>
<td colspan="2" style="background: #f3943f;">Strong</td>
<td colspan="2" style="background: #ea5297;">Violent</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2"></td>
<td colspan="4" style="background: #ffce44;">Significant</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="3"></td>
<td colspan="3" style="background: #e84e0f;">Intense</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The Fujita scale and the Enhanced Fujita Scale rate tornadoes by damage caused. The Enhanced Fujita (EF) Scale was an update to the older Fujita scale, by <a href="/wiki/Expert_elicitation" title="Expert elicitation">expert elicitation</a>, using engineered wind estimates and better damage descriptions. The EF Scale was designed so that a tornado rated on the Fujita scale would receive the same numerical rating, and was implemented starting in the United States in 2007. An EF0 tornado will probably damage trees but not substantial structures, whereas an EF5 tornado can rip buildings off their foundations leaving them bare and even deform large <a href="/wiki/Skyscraper" title="Skyscraper">skyscrapers</a>. The similar TORRO scale ranges from a T0 for extremely weak tornadoes to T11 for the most powerful known tornadoes. <a href="/wiki/Pulse-Doppler_radar" title="Pulse-Doppler radar">Doppler</a> <a href="/wiki/Weather_radar" title="Weather radar">weather radar</a> data, <a href="/wiki/Photogrammetry" title="Photogrammetry">photogrammetry</a>, and ground swirl patterns (<a href="/wiki/Cycloid" title="Cycloid">cycloidal</a> marks) may also be analyzed to determine intensity and award a rating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPC_FAQ_5-9"><a href="#cite_note-SPC_FAQ-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EF1_tornado_damage_example.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="438" data-file-width="640" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/EF1_tornado_damage_example.jpg/220px-EF1_tornado_damage_example.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/EF1_tornado_damage_example.jpg/330px-EF1_tornado_damage_example.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/EF1_tornado_damage_example.jpg/440px-EF1_tornado_damage_example.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EF1_tornado_damage_example.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A house displaying <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enhanced_Fujita_Scale" title="Enhanced Fujita Scale">EF1</a> damage. The roof and <a href="/wiki/Garage_door" title="Garage door">garage door</a> have been damaged, but walls and supporting structures are still intact.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Tornadoes vary in intensity regardless of shape, size, and location, though strong tornadoes are typically larger than weak tornadoes. The association with track length and duration also varies, although longer track tornadoes tend to be stronger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-width.2Flength_intensity_relationship_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-width.2Flength_intensity_relationship-64">[64]</a></sup> In the case of violent tornadoes, only a small portion of the path is of violent intensity, most of the higher intensity from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multiple_vortex_tornado" title="Multiple vortex tornado">subvortices</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-significant_tornadoes_22-6"><a href="#cite_note-significant_tornadoes-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the United States, 80% of tornadoes are EF0 and EF1 (T0 through T3) tornadoes. The rate of occurrence drops off quickly with increasing strength—less than 1% are violent tornadoes (EF4, T8 or stronger).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Basic_Spotter_Guide_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Basic_Spotter_Guide-65">[65]</a></sup> Outside <a href="/wiki/Tornado_Alley" title="Tornado Alley">Tornado Alley</a>, and North America in general, violent tornadoes are extremely rare. This is apparently mostly due to the lesser number of tornadoes overall, as research shows that tornado intensity distributions are fairly similar worldwide. A few significant tornadoes occur annually in Europe, Asia, southern Africa, and southeastern South America, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intensity_distribution_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-intensity_distribution-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climatology">Climatology</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tornado_climatology" title="Tornado climatology">Tornado climatology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Globdisttornado.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1465" data-file-width="2729" height="118" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Globdisttornado.jpg/220px-Globdisttornado.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Globdisttornado.jpg/330px-Globdisttornado.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Globdisttornado.jpg/440px-Globdisttornado.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Globdisttornado.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Areas worldwide where tornadoes are most likely, indicated by orange shading</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The United States has the most tornadoes of any country, nearly four times more than estimated in all of Europe, excluding waterspouts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-European_tornado_climatology_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-European_tornado_climatology-67">[67]</a></sup> This is mostly due to the unique geography of the continent. North America is a large continent that extends from the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a> north into <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">arctic</a> areas, and has no major east-west mountain range to block air flow between these two areas. In the <a href="/wiki/Middle_latitudes" title="Middle latitudes">middle latitudes</a>, where most tornadoes of the world occur, the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a> block moisture and buckle the <a href="/wiki/Prevailing_winds" title="Prevailing winds">atmospheric flow</a>, forcing drier air at mid-levels of the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a> due to downsloped winds, and causing <a href="/wiki/Cyclogenesis" title="Cyclogenesis">the formation of a low pressure area</a> downwind to the east of the mountains. Increased westerly flow off the Rockies force the formation of a <a href="/wiki/Dry_line" title="Dry line">dry line</a> when the flow aloft is strong,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> fuels abundant low-level moisture in the southerly flow to its east. This unique topography allows for frequent collisions of warm and cold air, the conditions that breed strong, long-lived storms throughout the year. A large portion of these tornadoes form in an area of the <a href="/wiki/Central_United_States" title="Central United States">central United States</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Tornado_Alley" title="Tornado Alley">Tornado Alley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_News_1_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Science_News_1-7">[7]</a></sup> This area extends into <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prairie_Provinces" title="Prairie Provinces">Prairie Provinces</a>, although southeast <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>, the interior of <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a>, and western <a href="/wiki/New_Brunswick" title="New Brunswick">New Brunswick</a> are also tornado-prone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Tornadoes also occur across northeastern Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPC_FAQ_5-10"><a href="#cite_note-SPC_FAQ-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>The United States averages about 1,200 tornadoes per year, followed by Canada, averaging 62 reported per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canada62_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canada62-70">[70]</a></sup> NOAA's has a higher average 100 per year in Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canada100_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canada100-71">[71]</a></sup> The Netherlands has the highest average number of recorded tornadoes per area of any country (more than 20, or 0.0013 per sq mi (0.00048 per km<sup>2</sup>), annually), followed by the UK (around 33, or 0.00035 per sq mi (0.00013 per km<sup>2</sup>), per year), although those are of lower intensity, briefer<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> and cause minor damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-European_tornado_climatology_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-European_tornado_climatology-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tornado_Alley.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="582" data-file-width="742" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Tornado_Alley.gif/220px-Tornado_Alley.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Tornado_Alley.gif/330px-Tornado_Alley.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Tornado_Alley.gif/440px-Tornado_Alley.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tornado_Alley.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Intense tornado activity in the United States. The darker-colored areas denote the area commonly referred to as <a href="/wiki/Tornado_Alley" title="Tornado Alley">Tornado Alley</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Tornadoes kill an average of 179 people per year in <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, the most in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bangladesh_tornado_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bangladesh_tornado-74">[74]</a></sup> Reasons for this include the region's high population density, poor construction quality, and lack of tornado safety knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bangladesh_tornado_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bangladesh_tornado-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Other areas of the world that have frequent tornadoes include South Africa, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/La_Plata_Basin" title="La Plata Basin">La Plata Basin</a> area, portions of Europe, Australia and New Zealand, and far eastern Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB_tornado_climatology_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-EB_tornado_climatology-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Tornadoes are most common in spring and least common in winter, but tornadoes can occur any time of year that favorable conditions occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-significant_tornadoes_22-7"><a href="#cite_note-significant_tornadoes-22">[22]</a></sup> Spring and fall experience peaks of activity as those are the seasons when stronger winds, wind shear, and atmospheric instability are present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LouieLouie_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-LouieLouie-77">[77]</a></sup> Tornadoes are focused in the right front quadrant of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landfall_(meteorology)" title="Landfall (meteorology)">landfalling</a> tropical cyclones, which tend to occur in the late summer and autumn. Tornadoes can also be spawned as a result of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eyewall_mesovortices" title="Eyewall mesovortices">eyewall mesovortices</a>, which persist until landfall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOML_FAQ_L6_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_L6-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>Tornado occurrence is highly dependent on the time of day, because of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">solar heating</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_time_of_day_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_time_of_day-79">[79]</a></sup> Worldwide, most tornadoes occur in the late afternoon, between 3 pm and 7 pm local time, with a peak near 5 pm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Destructive tornadoes can occur at any time of day. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tupelo-Gainesville_tornado_outbreak" title="Tupelo-Gainesville tornado outbreak">Gainesville Tornado</a> of 1936, one of the deadliest tornadoes in history, occurred at 8:30 am local time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-significant_tornadoes_22-8"><a href="#cite_note-significant_tornadoes-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The United Kingdom has the highest incidence of tornadoes, measured by unit area of land, than any other country in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Unsettled conditions and weather fronts transverse the Islands at all times of the years and are responsible for spawning the tornadoes, which consequently form at all times of the year. The United Kingdom has at least 34 tornadoes per year and possibly as many as 50,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> more than any other country in the world relative to its land area. Most tornadoes in the United Kingdom are weak, but they are occasionally destructive. For example, the Birmingham tornado of 2005 and the London tornado of 2006. Both registered F2 on the Fujita scale and both caused significant damage and injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Associations_with_climate_and_climate_change">Associations with climate and climate change</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tornado-counts-1976-2011.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="616" data-file-width="499" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Tornado-counts-1976-2011.png/220px-Tornado-counts-1976-2011.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Tornado-counts-1976-2011.png/330px-Tornado-counts-1976-2011.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Tornado-counts-1976-2011.png/440px-Tornado-counts-1976-2011.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tornado-counts-1976-2011.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
U. S. Annual January - December Tornado Count 1976–2011 from NOAA National Climatic Data Center</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Associations with various <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> and environmental trends exist. For example, an increase in the <a href="/wiki/Sea_surface_temperature" title="Sea surface temperature">sea surface temperature</a> of a source region (e.g. Gulf of Mexico and <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>) increases atmospheric moisture content. Increased moisture can fuel an increase in <a href="/wiki/Severe_weather" title="Severe weather">severe weather</a> and tornado activity, particularly in the cool season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_GoM_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards_GoM-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some evidence does suggest that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Oscillation" title="Southern Oscillation">Southern Oscillation</a> is weakly correlated with changes in tornado activity, which vary by season and region, as well as whether the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ENSO" title="ENSO">ENSO</a> phase is that of <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" title="El Niño">El Niño</a> or <a href="/wiki/La_Ni%C3%B1a" title="La Niña">La Niña</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AGU_ENSO_tor_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-AGU_ENSO_tor-89">[89]</a></sup> Research has found that fewer tornadoes and hailstorms occur in winter and spring in the U.S. central and southern plains during <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" title="El Niño">El Niño</a>, and more occur during <a href="/wiki/La_Ni%C3%B1a" title="La Niña">La Niña</a>, than in years when temperatures in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific" title="Pacific">Pacific</a> are relatively stable. Ocean conditions could be used to forecast extreme spring storm events several months in advance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Climatic shifts may affect tornadoes via <a href="/wiki/Teleconnection" title="Teleconnection">teleconnections</a> in shifting the jet stream and the larger weather patterns. The climate-tornado link is confounded by the forces affecting larger patterns and by the local, nuanced nature of tornadoes. Although it is reasonable to suspect that <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> may affect trends in tornado activity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agw_tstm_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-agw_tstm-91">[91]</a></sup> any such effect is not yet identifiable due to the complexity, local nature of the storms, and database quality issues. Any effect would vary by region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC4-WGI_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC4-WGI-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Detection">Detection</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tornado1857.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="1024" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Tornado1857.jpg/220px-Tornado1857.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Tornado1857.jpg/330px-Tornado1857.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Tornado1857.jpg/440px-Tornado1857.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tornado1857.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Path of a tornado across Wisconsin on August 21, 1857</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Convective_storm_detection" title="Convective storm detection">Convective storm detection</a></div>
<p>Rigorous attempts to warn of tornadoes began in the United States in the mid-20th century. Before the 1950s, the only method of detecting a tornado was by someone seeing it on the ground. Often, news of a tornado would reach a local weather office after the storm. However, with the advent of weather radar, areas near a local office could get advance warning of severe weather. The first public <a href="/wiki/Tornado_warning" title="Tornado warning">tornado warnings</a> were issued in 1950 and the first <a href="/wiki/Tornado_watch" title="Tornado watch">tornado watches</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convective_outlooks" title="Convective outlooks">convective outlooks</a> in 1952. In 1953 it was confirmed that <a href="/wiki/Hook_echo" title="Hook echo">hook echoes</a> are associated with tornadoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> By recognizing these radar signatures, meteorologists could detect thunderstorms probably producing tornadoes from dozens of miles away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hook_echoes_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-hook_echoes-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radar">Radar</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Pulse-Doppler_radar" title="Pulse-Doppler radar">Pulse-Doppler radar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Weather_radar" title="Weather radar">weather radar</a></div>
<p>Today, most developed countries have a network of weather radars, which remains the main method of detecting signatures probably associated with tornadoes. In the United States and a few other countries, Doppler weather radar stations are used. These devices measure the velocity and radial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direction_(geometry,_geography)" title="Direction (geometry, geography)">direction</a> (towards or away from the radar) of the winds in a storm, and so can spot evidence of rotation in storms from more than one hundred miles (160 km) away. When storms are distant from a radar, only areas high within the storm are observed and the important areas below are not sampled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-airbusradar_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-airbusradar-95">[95]</a></sup> Data resolution also decreases with distance from the radar.
