Some meteorological situations leading to tornadogenesis are not readily detectable by radar and on occasion tornado development may occur more quickly than radar can complete a scan and send the batch of data. Doppler radar systems can detect <a href="/wiki/Mesocyclone" title="Mesocyclone">mesocyclones</a> within the supercell of a thunderstorm. This allows meteorologists to predict tornado formations throughout thunderstorms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:702px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:05june-rapiddow-wide.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="832" data-file-width="1914" height="304" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/05june-rapiddow-wide.gif/700px-05june-rapiddow-wide.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/05june-rapiddow-wide.gif/1050px-05june-rapiddow-wide.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/05june-rapiddow-wide.gif/1400px-05june-rapiddow-wide.gif 2x" width="700"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:05june-rapiddow-wide.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Doppler_on_Wheels" title="Doppler on Wheels">Doppler on Wheels</a> radar loop of a <a href="/wiki/Hook_echo" title="Hook echo">hook echo</a> and associated mesocyclone in <a href="/wiki/Tornadoes_of_2009#June_5" title="Tornadoes of 2009">Goshen County, Wyoming on June 5, 2009</a>. Strong mesocyclones show up as adjacent areas of yellow and blue (on other radars, bright red and bright green), and usually indicate an imminent or occurring tornado.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Storm_spotting">Storm spotting</span></h3>
<p>In the mid-1970s, the U.S. National Weather Service (NWS) increased its efforts to train <a href="/wiki/Storm_spotting" title="Storm spotting">storm spotters</a> to spot key features of storms which indicate severe hail, damaging winds, and tornadoes, as well as damage itself and <a href="/wiki/Flash_flood" title="Flash flood">flash flooding</a>. The program was called <a href="/wiki/Skywarn" title="Skywarn">Skywarn</a>, and the spotters were local sheriff's deputies, state troopers, firefighters, ambulance drivers, <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio_operator" title="Amateur radio operator">amateur radio operators</a>, <a href="/wiki/Civil_defense" title="Civil defense">civil defense</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Emergency_management" title="Emergency management">emergency management</a>) spotters, <a href="/wiki/Storm_chasing" title="Storm chasing">storm chasers</a>, and ordinary citizens. When severe weather is anticipated, local weather service offices request that these spotters look out for severe weather, and report any tornadoes immediately, so that the office can warn of the hazard.</p>
<p>Usually spotters are trained by the NWS on behalf of their respective organizations, and report to them. The organizations activate public warning systems such as <a href="/wiki/Civil_defense_siren" title="Civil defense siren">sirens</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Emergency_Alert_System" title="Emergency Alert System">Emergency Alert System</a> (EAS), and forward the report to the NWS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotter_history_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-spotter_history-97">[97]</a></sup> There are more than 230,000 trained Skywarn weather spotters across the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWS_SKYWARN_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-NWS_SKYWARN-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Canada, a similar network of volunteer weather watchers, called <a href="/wiki/Canwarn" title="Canwarn">Canwarn</a>, helps spot severe weather, with more than 1,000 volunteers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-environment_Canada_detection_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-environment_Canada_detection-99">[99]</a></sup> In Europe, several nations are organizing spotter networks under the auspices of <a href="/wiki/Skywarn_Europe" title="Skywarn Europe">Skywarn Europe</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> and the Tornado and Storm Research Organisation (TORRO) has maintained a network of spotters in the United Kingdom since 1974.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>Storm spotters are needed because radar systems such as <a href="/wiki/NEXRAD" title="NEXRAD">NEXRAD</a> do not detect a tornado; merely signatures which hint at the presence of tornadoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSLsearch_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSLsearch-102">[102]</a></sup> Radar may give a warning before there is any visual evidence of a tornado or imminent tornado, but <a href="/wiki/Ground_truth" title="Ground truth">ground truth</a> from an observer can either verify the threat or determine that a tornado is not imminent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> The spotter's ability to see what radar cannot is especially important as distance from the radar site increases, because the radar beam becomes progressively higher in altitude further away from the radar, chiefly due to curvature of Earth, and the beam also spreads out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-airbusradar_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-airbusradar-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visual_evidence">Visual evidence</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wall_cloud12_-_NOAA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1180" data-file-width="1836" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Wall_cloud12_-_NOAA.jpg/220px-Wall_cloud12_-_NOAA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Wall_cloud12_-_NOAA.jpg/330px-Wall_cloud12_-_NOAA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Wall_cloud12_-_NOAA.jpg/440px-Wall_cloud12_-_NOAA.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wall_cloud12_-_NOAA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A rotating <a href="/wiki/Wall_cloud" title="Wall cloud">wall cloud</a> with <a href="/wiki/Rear_flank_downdraft" title="Rear flank downdraft">rear flank downdraft</a> clear slot evident to its left rear</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Storm spotters are trained to discern whether a storm seen from a distance is a supercell. They typically look to its rear, the main region of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Updraft" title="Updraft">updraft</a> and inflow. Under the updraft is a rain-free base, and the next step of <a href="/wiki/Tornadogenesis" title="Tornadogenesis">tornadogenesis</a> is the formation of a rotating <a href="/wiki/Wall_cloud" title="Wall cloud">wall cloud</a>. The vast majority of intense tornadoes occur with a wall cloud on the backside of a supercell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Basic_Spotter_Guide_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Basic_Spotter_Guide-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Evidence of a supercell comes from the storm's shape and structure, and cloud tower features such as a hard and vigorous updraft tower, a persistent, large <a href="/wiki/Overshooting_top" title="Overshooting top">overshooting top</a>, a hard anvil (especially when backsheared against strong upper level <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">winds</a>), and a corkscrew look or <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Striation_(meteorology)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Striation (meteorology) (page does not exist)">striations</a>. Under the storm and closer to where most tornadoes are found, evidence of a supercell and likelihood of a tornado includes inflow bands (particularly when curved) such as a "beaver tail", and other clues such as strength of inflow, warmth and moistness of inflow air, how outflow- or inflow-dominant a storm appears, and how far is the front flank precipitation core from the wall cloud. Tornadogenesis is most likely at the interface of the updraft and <a href="/wiki/Rear_flank_downdraft" title="Rear flank downdraft">rear flank downdraft</a>, and requires a balance between the outflow and inflow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Advanced_Spotter_Guide_17-7"><a href="#cite_note-Advanced_Spotter_Guide-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Only wall clouds that rotate spawn tornadoes, and usually precede the tornado by five to thirty minutes. Rotating wall clouds may be a visual manifestation of a low-level mesocyclone. Barring a low-level boundary, tornadogenesis is highly unlikely unless a rear flank downdraft occurs, which is usually visibly evidenced by evaporation of <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">cloud</a> adjacent to a corner of a wall cloud. A tornado often occurs as this happens or shortly after; first, a funnel cloud dips and in nearly all cases by the time it reaches halfway down, a surface swirl has already developed, signifying a tornado is on the ground before condensation connects the surface circulation to the storm. Tornadoes may also occur without wall clouds, under flanking lines, and on the leading edge. Spotters watch all areas of a storm, and the <a href="/wiki/Cloud_base" title="Cloud base">cloud base</a> and surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSL_tornadoes_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSL_tornadoes-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extremes">Extremes</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tornado_records" title="Tornado records">Tornado records</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Super_Outbreak_Map.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1631" data-file-width="1345" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Super_Outbreak_Map.jpg/220px-Super_Outbreak_Map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Super_Outbreak_Map.jpg/330px-Super_Outbreak_Map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Super_Outbreak_Map.jpg/440px-Super_Outbreak_Map.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Super_Outbreak_Map.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A map of the tornado paths in the Super Outbreak (April 3–4, 1974)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The most record-breaking tornado in recorded history was the <a href="/wiki/Tri-State_Tornado" title="Tri-State Tornado">Tri-State Tornado</a>, which roared through parts of <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a> on March 18, 1925. It was likely an F5, though tornadoes were not ranked on any scale in that era. It holds records for longest path length (219 miles; 352 km), longest duration (about 3.5 hours), and fastest forward speed for a significant tornado (73 mph; 117 km/h) anywhere on Earth. In addition, it is the deadliest single tornado in United States history (695 dead).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-significant_tornadoes_22-9"><a href="#cite_note-significant_tornadoes-22">[22]</a></sup> The tornado was also the costliest tornado in history at the time (unadjusted for inflation), but in the years since has been surpassed by several others if population changes over time are not considered. When costs are normalized for wealth and inflation, it ranks third today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_damage_cost_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_damage_cost-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>The deadliest tornado in world history was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daultipur-Salturia_Tornado" title="Daultipur-Salturia Tornado">Daultipur-Salturia Tornado</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> on April 26, 1989, which killed approximately 1,300 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bangladesh_tornado_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bangladesh_tornado-74">[74]</a></sup> Bangladesh has had at least 19 tornadoes in its history kill more than 100 people, almost half of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tornadoes_causing_100_or_more_deaths" title="List of tornadoes causing 100 or more deaths">total in the rest of the world</a>.</p>
<p>The most extensive <a href="/wiki/Tornado_outbreak" title="Tornado outbreak">tornado outbreak</a> on record was the <a href="/wiki/2011_Super_Outbreak" title="2011 Super Outbreak">2011 Super Outbreak</a>, which spawned 362 confirmed tornadoes over the southeastern United States–218 of them within a single 24-hour period. The previous record was the <a href="/wiki/1974_Super_Outbreak" title="1974 Super Outbreak">1974 Super Outbreak</a> which spawned 148 tornadoes.</p>
<p>While direct measurement of the most violent tornado wind speeds is nearly impossible, since conventional <a href="/wiki/Anemometer" title="Anemometer">anemometers</a> would be destroyed by the intense winds and flying debris, some tornadoes have been scanned by <a href="/wiki/Doppler_on_Wheels" title="Doppler on Wheels">mobile Doppler radar units</a>, which can provide a good estimate of the tornado's winds. The highest wind speed ever measured in a tornado, which is also the highest wind speed ever recorded on the planet, is 301 ± 20 mph (484 ± 32 km/h) in the F5 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1999_Bridge_Creek_%E2%80%93_Moore_tornado" title="1999 Bridge Creek – Moore tornado">Bridge Creek-Moore, Oklahoma</a>, tornado which killed 36 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Though the reading was taken about 100 feet (30 m) above the ground, this is a testament to the power of the strongest tornadoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fastest_wind_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-fastest_wind-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Storms that produce tornadoes can feature intense updrafts, sometimes exceeding 150 mph (240 km/h). Debris from a tornado can be lofted into the parent storm and carried a very long distance. A tornado which affected <a href="/wiki/Great_Bend,_Kansas" title="Great Bend, Kansas">Great Bend, Kansas</a>, in November 1915, was an extreme case, where a "rain of debris" occurred 80 miles (130 km) from the town, a sack of flour was found 110 miles (180 km) away, and a cancelled check from the Great Bend bank was found in a field outside of <a href="/wiki/Palmyra,_Nebraska" title="Palmyra, Nebraska">Palmyra, Nebraska</a>, 305 miles (491 km) to the northeast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_project_oddities_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_project_oddities-107">[107]</a></sup> Waterspouts and tornadoes have been advanced as an explanation for instances of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raining_animals" title="Raining animals">raining fish and other animals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tornado_preparedness" title="Tornado preparedness">Tornado preparedness</a></div>
<p>Though tornadoes can strike in an instant, there are precautions and preventative measures that people can take to increase the chances of surviving a tornado. Authorities such as the <a href="/wiki/Storm_Prediction_Center" title="Storm Prediction Center">Storm Prediction Center</a> advise having a pre-determined plan should a tornado warning be issued. When a warning is issued, going to a basement or an interior first-floor room of a sturdy building greatly increases chances of survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_safety_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_safety-109">[109]</a></sup> In tornado-prone areas, many buildings have <a href="/wiki/Storm_cellar" title="Storm cellar">storm cellars</a> on the property. These underground refuges have saved thousands of lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some countries have meteorological agencies which distribute tornado forecasts and increase levels of alert of a possible tornado (such as <a href="/wiki/Tornado_watch" title="Tornado watch">tornado watches</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tornado_warning" title="Tornado warning">warnings</a> in the United States and Canada). <a href="/wiki/Weather_radio" title="Weather radio">Weather radios</a> provide an alarm when a severe weather advisory is issued for the local area, though these are mainly available only in the United States. Unless the tornado is far away and highly visible, meteorologists advise that drivers park their vehicles far to the side of the road (so as not to block emergency traffic), and find a sturdy shelter. If no sturdy shelter is nearby, getting low in a ditch is the next best option. Highway overpasses are one of the worst places to take shelter during tornadoes, as the constricted space can be subject to increased wind speed and funneling of debris underneath the overpass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-highway_overpasses_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-highway_overpasses-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Myths_and_misconceptions">Myths and misconceptions</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tornado_myths" title="Tornado myths">Tornado myths</a></div>
<p>Folklore often identifies a green sky with tornadoes, and though the phenomenon may be associated with severe weather, there is no evidence linking it specifically with tornadoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> It is often thought that opening windows will lessen the damage caused by the tornado. While there is a large drop in <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">atmospheric pressure</a> inside a strong tornado, it is unlikely that the pressure drop would be enough to cause the house to explode. Opening windows may actually increase the severity of the tornado's damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_project_myths_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_project_myths-113">[113]</a></sup> A violent tornado can destroy a house whether its windows are open or closed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_project_myths_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_project_myths-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_myths_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_myths-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1999_Salt_Lake_City_tornado.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="242" data-file-width="320" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/1999_Salt_Lake_City_tornado.jpg/220px-1999_Salt_Lake_City_tornado.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3e/1999_Salt_Lake_City_tornado.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1999_Salt_Lake_City_tornado.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/1999_Salt_Lake_City_tornado" title="1999 Salt Lake City tornado">1999 Salt Lake City tornado</a> disproved several misconceptions, including the idea that tornadoes cannot occur in cities.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Another commonly held misconception is that highway overpasses provide adequate shelter from tornadoes. This belief is partly inspired by widely circulated video captured during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/April_26,_1991_tornado_outbreak" title="April 26, 1991 tornado outbreak">1991 tornado outbreak</a> near <a href="/wiki/Andover,_Kansas" title="Andover, Kansas">Andover, Kansas</a>, where a news crew and several other people take shelter under an overpass on the <a href="/wiki/Kansas_Turnpike" title="Kansas Turnpike">Kansas Turnpike</a> and safely ride out a tornado as it passes by.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaa-overpass_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-noaa-overpass-115">[115]</a></sup> However, a highway overpass is a dangerous place during a tornado: the subjects of the video remained safe due to an unlikely combination of events: the storm in question was a weak tornado, did not directly strike the overpass, and the overpass itself was of a unique design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaa-overpass_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-noaa-overpass-115">[115]</a></sup> Due to the <a href="/wiki/Venturi_effect" title="Venturi effect">Venturi effect</a>, tornadic winds are accelerated in the confined space of an overpass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Indeed, in the <a href="/wiki/1999_Oklahoma_tornado_outbreak" title="1999 Oklahoma tornado outbreak">1999 Oklahoma tornado outbreak</a> of May 3, 1999, three highway overpasses were directly struck by tornadoes, and at all three locations there was a fatality, along with many life-threatening injuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-highway_overpass_danger_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-highway_overpass_danger-117">[117]</a></sup> By comparison, during the same tornado outbreak, more than 2000 homes were completely destroyed, with another 7000 damaged, and yet only a few dozen people died in their homes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-highway_overpasses_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-highway_overpasses-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>An old belief is that the southwest corner of a basement provides the most protection during a tornado. The safest place is the side or corner of an underground room opposite the tornado's direction of approach (usually the northeast corner), or the central-most room on the lowest floor. Taking shelter in a basement, under a staircase, or under a sturdy piece of furniture such as a workbench further increases chances of survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_project_myths_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_project_myths-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_myths_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_myths-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>Finally, there are areas which people believe to be protected from tornadoes, whether by being in a city, near a major river, hill, or mountain, or even protected by <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Tornadoes have been known to cross major rivers, climb mountains,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tornadoes_in_mountains_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tornadoes_in_mountains-119">[119]</a></sup> affect valleys, and have damaged <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tornadoes_striking_downtown_areas" title="List of tornadoes striking downtown areas">several city centers</a>. As a general rule, no area is safe from tornadoes, though some areas are more susceptible than others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handy_Weather_Answer_Book_24-6"><a href="#cite_note-Handy_Weather_Answer_Book-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_project_myths_113-3"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_project_myths-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_myths_114-2"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_myths-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ongoing_research">Ongoing research</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tornado_with_DOW.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="332" data-file-width="500" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Tornado_with_DOW.jpg/220px-Tornado_with_DOW.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Tornado_with_DOW.jpg/330px-Tornado_with_DOW.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Tornado_with_DOW.jpg/440px-Tornado_with_DOW.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tornado_with_DOW.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Doppler_on_Wheels" title="Doppler on Wheels">Doppler on Wheels</a> unit observing a tornado near <a href="/wiki/Attica,_Kansas" title="Attica, Kansas">Attica, Kansas</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Meteorology is a relatively young science and the study of tornadoes is newer still. Although researched for about 140 years and intensively for around 60 years, there are still aspects of tornadoes which remain a mystery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VORTEX_book_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-VORTEX_book-120">[120]</a></sup> Scientists have a fairly good understanding of the development of <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">thunderstorms</a> and mesocyclones,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Extreme_Weather_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Extreme_Weather-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> and the meteorological conditions conducive to their formation. However, the step from <a href="/wiki/Supercell" title="Supercell">supercell</a>, or other respective formative processes, to <a href="/wiki/Tornadogenesis" title="Tornadogenesis">tornadogenesis</a> and the prediction of tornadic vs. non-tornadic mesocyclones is not yet well known and is the focus of much research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LouieLouie_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-LouieLouie-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>Also under study are the low-level mesocyclone and the <a href="/wiki/Vortex_stretching" title="Vortex stretching">stretching</a> of low-level <a href="/wiki/Vorticity" title="Vorticity">vorticity</a> which tightens into a tornado,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LouieLouie_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-LouieLouie-77">[77]</a></sup> in particular, what are the processes and what is the relationship of the environment and the convective storm. Intense tornadoes have been observed forming simultaneously with a mesocyclone aloft (rather than succeeding mesocyclogenesis) and some intense tornadoes have occurred without a mid-level mesocyclone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seymour_2001_32_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seymour_2001_32-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>In particular, the role of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downdraft" title="Downdraft">downdrafts</a>, particularly the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rear-flank_downdraft" title="Rear-flank downdraft">rear-flank downdraft</a>, and the role of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baroclinic" title="Baroclinic">baroclinic</a> boundaries, are intense areas of study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>Reliably predicting tornado intensity and longevity remains a problem, as do details affecting characteristics of a tornado during its life cycle and tornadolysis. Other rich areas of research are tornadoes associated with <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mesovortex" title="wikt:mesovortex">mesovortices</a> within linear thunderstorm structures and within tropical cyclones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_forecasting_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_forecasting-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>Scientists still do not know the exact mechanisms by which most tornadoes form, and occasional tornadoes still strike without a tornado warning being issued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Analysis of observations including both stationary and mobile (surface and aerial) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In-situ" title="In-situ">in-situ</a> and <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing" title="Remote sensing">remote sensing</a> (passive and active) instruments generates new ideas and refines existing notions. <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_model" title="Mathematical model">Numerical modeling</a> also provides new insights as observations and new discoveries are integrated into our physical understanding and then tested in <a href="/wiki/Computer_simulation" title="Computer simulation">computer simulations</a> which validate new notions as well as produce entirely new theoretical findings, many of which are otherwise unattainable. Importantly, development of new observation technologies and installation of finer spatial and temporal resolution observation networks have aided increased understanding and better predictions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>Research programs, including field projects such as the <a href="/wiki/VORTEX_projects" title="VORTEX projects">VORTEX projects</a> (Verification of the Origins of Rotation in Tornadoes Experiment), deployment of <a href="/wiki/TOtable_Tornado_Observatory" title="TOtable Tornado Observatory">TOTO</a> (the TOtable Tornado Observatory), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doppler_On_Wheels" title="Doppler On Wheels">Doppler On Wheels</a> (DOW), and dozens of other programs, hope to solve many questions that still plague meteorologists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-field_programs_history_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-field_programs_history-45">[45]</a></sup> Universities, government agencies such as the <a href="/wiki/National_Severe_Storms_Laboratory" title="National Severe Storms Laboratory">National Severe Storms Laboratory</a>, private-sector meteorologists, and the <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Atmospheric_Research" title="National Center for Atmospheric Research">National Center for Atmospheric Research</a> are some of the organizations very active in research; with various sources of funding, both private and public, a chief entity being the <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">National Science Foundation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSLsearch_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-NSSLsearch-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> The pace of research is partly constrained by the number of observations that can be taken; gaps in information about the wind, pressure, and moisture content throughout the local atmosphere; and the computing power available for simulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>Solar storms similar to tornadoes have been recorded, but it is unknown how closely related they are to their terrestrial counterparts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">

St. Johnsbury, VT: Environmental Films. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1-879362-04-X" title="Special:BookSources/1-879362-04-X">1-879362-04-X</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATornado&amp;rft.au=Thomas+P.+Grazulis&amp;rft.btitle=Significant+Tornadoes+Update%2C+1992%E2%80%931995&amp;rft.date=1997-01&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=1-879362-04-X&amp;rft.place=St.+Johnsbury%2C+VT&amp;rft.pub=Environmental+Films&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
<li>Pybus, Nani, "'Cyclone' Jones: Dr. Herbert L. Jones and the Origins of Tornado Research in Oklahoma," <i>Chronicles of Oklahoma</i> 94 (Spring 2016), 4–31. Heavily illustrated.</li>
</ul>
